ولا خفاء في أن هذا لا يكفي فيما ثبت بالضرورة من الدين. . في طريقة الفلاسفة بالاحتياج إلى النبي والشريعة (١)، وبثبوت المعجزة، لكن يقررون ذلك على وجه لا يوافق ما علم بالضرورة من الدين . أما تقريرهم في الاحتياج الى النبي فهو أن الإنسان مدني بالطبع ، أي محتاج في تعيشه إلى التمدن وهو اجتماعه مع بني نوعه للتعاون والتشارك في تحصيل ما يحتاجون إليه من الغذاء الموافق واللباس الواقي من الحر والبرد والمسكن الملائم بحسب الفصول المختلفة، والسلاح الحامي عن السباع والأعداء، فإن كل ذلك مما يحصل بالصناعات، ولا يمكن للإنسان الواحد القيام بجميعها، بل لا بد أن يخبز هذا لذلك، وذلك يخيط لآخر ، وآخر يتخذ الإبرة له إلى غير ذلك المصالح التي لا بقاء للنوع (۲) بدونها ، ثم ذلك التعاون والتشارك لا يتم إلا بمعاملات فيما بينهم ومعاوضات، ولا ينتظم إلا بقانون متفق عليه مبني على العدل والإنصاف، ضابط لما لا حصر له من الجزئيات، لئلا يقع الجور ، فيختل أمر النظام لما جبل عليه كل أحد من أنه يشتهي ما يحتاج إليه، ويغضب على من (۱) الشريعة : هي الائتمار بالتزام العبودية ، وقيل الشريعة هي الطريق في الدين . (۲) النوع : اسم دال على أشياء كثيرة مختلفة بالأشخاص والنوع الحقيقي كل مقول على واحد أو على كثيرين متفقين بالحقائق في جواب ما هو فالكلي: جنس، والمقول على واحد إشارة إلى النوع المنحصر في الشخص ، والنوع الإضافي : هي ماهية يقال لها وعلى غيرها الجنس قولاً أولياً أي بلا واسطة كالإنسان بالقياس إلى الحيوان فإنه ماهية يقال عليها وعلى غيرها كالفرس الجنس، وهو الحيوان، حتى إذا قيل ما الإنسان والفرس، فالجواب أنه حيوان، وهذا المعنى يسمى نوعاً إضافياً لأن نوعيته بالإضافة إلى ما فوقه وهو الحيوان والجسم النامي، والجسم والجوهر.
He said: The Third Investigation: The philosophers said: Man, in his living, needs association with his own kind, and cooperation which is not completed except through transactions and exchanges that require an agreed-upon law established by one who is distinguished from others by a special quality from the Creator of all, and signs that necessitate acknowledgment of him and submission to him. These are, according to the human power, knowledge of unseen things through the soul's connection to the world of the unseen; and according to the animal power, by way of movements, the appearance of actions that his likes are incapable of, such as the occurrence of winds, earthquakes, burning, drowning, destruction of oppressive persons and corrupt cities, and the like, due to the soul's special capacity to act upon bodies other than its own; and by way of rests, abstaining from food for an unusual period due to the soul's attraction to the world of holiness and its subjugation of the nutritive power and its servants. From here, it is possible that the perfect intellects and celestial souls, especially the Active Intellect which has an additional special connection to the elemental world, appear to his complete imaginative power as forms that speak to him and produce in his hearing organized speech that is memorized and recited. This is revelation, and the descent of the angel and the scripture. As for that being from Allah for the order of livelihood, salvation of the afterlife, and rectification of servants, while negating intent and purpose from His actions and knowledge of the particular in a particular manner in His attributes, they established it by saying: Divine care—meaning the encompassment of His prior knowledge of the system of existents in a fitting manner—necessitates the emanation of that system in the order and detail which includes the existence of the law and the lawgiver so that the existent may be in accordance with the known. There is no concealment that this does not suffice for what is established by necessity from religion. In the philosophers' method of the need for a prophet and a law (1), and the establishment of a miracle, they present it in a manner that does not agree with what is known by necessity from religion. As for their presentation of the need for a prophet, it is that man is civil by nature—meaning he needs, in his living, civilization, which is his association with his own kind for cooperation and sharing in obtaining what they need of suitable food, clothing protecting from heat and cold, appropriate housing according to different seasons, and weapons protecting from beasts and enemies. All of that is acquired through crafts, and a single person cannot undertake all of them; rather, this one must bake for that one, that one sew for another, another make the needle for him, and so on for other interests without which the species (2) cannot survive. Then this cooperation and sharing are not completed except through transactions and exchanges among them, and they are not orderly except by an agreed-upon law based on justice and fairness, regulating the countless particulars, lest injustice occur and the order be disturbed, because each person is naturally disposed to desire what he needs and to become angry at (1) Al-Shari'a: It is obeying commitment to servitude, and it is said al-Shari'a is the way in religion. (2) Al-Naw': A noun indicating many things differing by individuals. The real species is everything predicated of one or many agreeing in realities in answer to 'what is it'; the universal is genus, and the predication of one indicates the species confined to the person. The relative species is an essence predicated of it and of others by the genus primarily, i.e., without intermediary, like man in relation to animal—it is an essence predicated of it and of others like the horse by genus, which is animal, so when asked 'what is man and horse?', the answer is 'animal'. This meaning is called relative species because its species-ness is relative to what is above it, which is animal, growing body, body, substance.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is the Third Investigation (المبحث الثالث) presenting the philosophers' (الحكماء) argument for the necessity of prophethood and law. It begins by stating the human need for social cooperation, which requires a lawgiver distinguished by special qualities from God and signs (miracles) that compel recognition. The philosophers explain that such signs derive from two sources: the human power to know the unseen via the soul's connection to the higher world, and the animal power to perform extraordinary actions (e.g., wind, earthquake, destruction) through the soul's control of external bodies, or to abstain from food by the soul's attraction to holiness. From this, revelation is possible when the Active Intellect appears to the prophet's imagination as speaking forms and produces organized speech. The philosophers then attribute this to divine care (العناية الإلهية)—the encompassment of God's prior knowledge—which necessitates the emanation of the system of existence, including a law and lawgiver, so that actual existence matches God's knowledge. The author comments that this is insufficient for what is known necessarily from religion. The passage then transitions to a detailed exposition of the philosophers' method: first, the need for a prophet stems from man's civil nature (الإنسان مدني بالطبع)—he needs society for food, clothing, shelter, defense—tasks requiring specialization; cooperation needs transactions and exchanges, which require an agreed law based on justice and fairness to regulate particulars. The footnote defines 'shari'a' and 'naw' (species), the latter explaining the logical term.

Key Terms

الحاجة إلى النبي
المعجزة
العناية الإلهية
القوة المتخيلة
العقل الفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the philosophers' argument is fully compatible with Islamic theology.
Correction: Taftazani explicitly states that the philosophers' basis (denial of divine purpose and particular knowledge) makes it incompatible with what is known necessarily from religion.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the primary function of the law established by a prophet?
A) To punish evildoers
B) To regulate transactions and exchanges for social cooperation
C) To create a hierarchy among humans
D) To enable miracles

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue for prophethood through human need for society and law, supported by a theory of miracles and divine care, but Taftazani judges this insufficient for religious certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the philosophers see prophethood as a necessity for social order, but they deny that God acts with purpose. When you follow the Prophet’s ﷺ teachings, do you ever treat them merely as useful rules for society rather than commands from a purposeful Lord? What would change in your obedience if you remembered that every command flows from God’s will and wisdom, not just from necessity?
?
The philosophers argue that the prophet’s signs come from the soul’s connection to higher realms. But Taftazani warns that this view is insufficient for religious certainty. Have you ever reduced the miracle of the Qur’an to a mere ‘spiritual experience’ or psychological phenomenon, without the awe of it being a direct act of God? How can you train your heart to see the Qur’an as a miracle sent by a Lord who knows and wills every detail?
?
If you were challenged to explain why you believe in prophethood, would you rely on rational arguments like the philosophers’ or on the certainty that comes from faith? Which gives you more conviction, and why?

Man's Civil Nature and Need for a Lawgiver
Detailed exposition of the philosophers' argument for prophethood based on human civil nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' detailed argument that humans are civil by nature, requiring specialization and a just law to regulate cooperation and prevent injustice.

Core Idea
Humans cannot survive alone; society needs a lawgiver to ensure justice.

In this part of the passage, Taftazani elaborates the philosophers' core reasoning: man is 'civil by nature' (مدني بالطبع), meaning he cannot fulfill his needs alone. Cooperation through specialization requires exchanges, which must be governed by a just, universally agreed law. This sets the stage for the necessity of a prophet as lawgiver.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'civil by nature'
The philosophers define man as naturally needing civilization, i.e., association with others for cooperation and sharing. This is the foundation.
This phrase is key.
2
List the basic needs
Identify the needs that cannot be met individually: food, clothing, housing, weapons. These require multiple crafts.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow from needs → specialization → cooperation → transactions → need for a just law. Each step relies on the previous.
4
Note the role of human nature
The passage mentions that humans naturally desire what they need and become angry when denied, necessitating a law to prevent injustice.
This psychological observation supports the need for a law.

الاحتياج الى النبي فهو أن الإنسان مدني بالطبع ، أي محتاج في تعيشه إلى التمدن وهو اجتماعه مع بني نوعه للتعاون والتشارك في تحصيل ما يحتاجون إليه من الغذاء الموافق واللباس الواقي من الحر والبرد والمسكن الملائم بحسب الفصول المختلفة، والسلاح الحامي عن السباع والأعداء، فإن كل ذلك مما يحصل بالصناعات، ولا يمكن للإنسان الواحد القيام بجميعها، بل لا بد أن يخبز هذا لذلك، وذلك يخيط لآخر ، وآخر يتخذ الإبرة له إلى غير ذلك المصالح التي لا بقاء للنوع (۲) بدونها ، ثم ذلك التعاون والتشارك لا يتم إلا بمعاملات فيما بينهم ومعاوضات، ولا ينتظم إلا بقانون متفق عليه مبني على العدل والإنصاف، ضابط لما لا حصر له من الجزئيات، لئلا يقع الجور ، فيختل أمر النظام لما جبل عليه كل أحد من أنه يشتهي ما يحتاج إليه، ويغضب على من (۱) الشريعة : هي الائتمار بالتزام العبودية ، وقيل الشريعة هي الطريق في الدين . (۲) النوع : اسم دال على أشياء كثيرة مختلفة بالأشخاص والنوع الحقيقي كل مقول على واحد أو على كثيرين متفقين بالحقائق في جواب ما هو فالكلي: جنس، والمقول على واحد إشارة إلى النوع المنحصر في الشخص ، والنوع الإضافي : هي ماهية يقال لها وعلى غيرها الجنس قولاً أولياً أي بلا واسطة كالإنسان بالقياس إلى الحيوان فإنه ماهية يقال عليها وعلى غيرها كالفرس الجنس، وهو الحيوان، حتى إذا قيل ما الإنسان والفرس، فالجواب أنه حيوان، وهذا المعنى يسمى نوعاً إضافياً لأن نوعيته بالإضافة إلى ما فوقه وهو الحيوان والجسم النامي، والجسم والجوهر.
He said: The Third Investigation: The philosophers said: Man, in his living, needs association with his own kind, and cooperation which is not completed except through transactions and exchanges that require an agreed-upon law established by one who is distinguished from others by a special quality from the Creator of all, and signs that necessitate acknowledgment of him and submission to him. These are, according to the human power, knowledge of unseen things through the soul's connection to the world of the unseen; and according to the animal power, by way of movements, the appearance of actions that his likes are incapable of, such as the occurrence of winds, earthquakes, burning, drowning, destruction of oppressive persons and corrupt cities, and the like, due to the soul's special capacity to act upon bodies other than its own; and by way of rests, abstaining from food for an unusual period due to the soul's attraction to the world of holiness and its subjugation of the nutritive power and its servants. From here, it is possible that the perfect intellects and celestial souls, especially the Active Intellect which has an additional special connection to the elemental world, appear to his complete imaginative power as forms that speak to him and produce in his hearing organized speech that is memorized and recited. This is revelation, and the descent of the angel and the scripture. As for that being from Allah for the order of livelihood, salvation of the afterlife, and rectification of servants, while negating intent and purpose from His actions and knowledge of the particular in a particular manner in His attributes, they established it by saying: Divine care—meaning the encompassment of His prior knowledge of the system of existents in a fitting manner—necessitates the emanation of that system in the order and detail which includes the existence of the law and the lawgiver so that the existent may be in accordance with the known. There is no concealment that this does not suffice for what is established by necessity from religion. In the philosophers' method of the need for a prophet and a law (1), and the establishment of a miracle, they present it in a manner that does not agree with what is known by necessity from religion. As for their presentation of the need for a prophet, it is that man is civil by nature—meaning he needs, in his living, civilization, which is his association with his own kind for cooperation and sharing in obtaining what they need of suitable food, clothing protecting from heat and cold, appropriate housing according to different seasons, and weapons protecting from beasts and enemies. All of that is acquired through crafts, and a single person cannot undertake all of them; rather, this one must bake for that one, that one sew for another, another make the needle for him, and so on for other interests without which the species (2) cannot survive. Then this cooperation and sharing are not completed except through transactions and exchanges among them, and they are not orderly except by an agreed-upon law based on justice and fairness, regulating the countless particulars, lest injustice occur and the order be disturbed, because each person is naturally disposed to desire what he needs and to become angry at (1) Al-Shari'a: It is obeying commitment to servitude, and it is said al-Shari'a is the way in religion. (2) Al-Naw': A noun indicating many things differing by individuals. The real species is everything predicated of one or many agreeing in realities in answer to 'what is it'; the universal is genus, and the predication of one indicates the species confined to the person. The relative species is an essence predicated of it and of others by the genus primarily, i.e., without intermediary, like man in relation to animal—it is an essence predicated of it and of others like the horse by genus, which is animal, so when asked 'what is man and horse?', the answer is 'animal'. This meaning is called relative species because its species-ness is relative to what is above it, which is animal, growing body, body, substance.

Commentary
This passage is the Third Investigation (المبحث الثالث) presenting the philosophers' (الحكماء) argument for the necessity of prophethood and law. It begins by stating the human need for social cooperation, which requires a lawgiver distinguished by special qualities from God and signs (miracles) that compel recognition. The philosophers explain that such signs derive from two sources: the human power to know the unseen via the soul's connection to the higher world, and the animal power to perform extraordinary actions (e.g., wind, earthquake, destruction) through the soul's control of external bodies, or to abstain from food by the soul's attraction to holiness. From this, revelation is possible when the Active Intellect appears to the prophet's imagination as speaking forms and produces organized speech. The philosophers then attribute this to divine care (العناية الإلهية)—the encompassment of God's prior knowledge—which necessitates the emanation of the system of existence, including a law and lawgiver, so that actual existence matches God's knowledge. The author comments that this is insufficient for what is known necessarily from religion. The passage then transitions to a detailed exposition of the philosophers' method: first, the need for a prophet stems from man's civil nature (الإنسان مدني بالطبع)—he needs society for food, clothing, shelter, defense—tasks requiring specialization; cooperation needs transactions and exchanges, which require an agreed law based on justice and fairness to regulate particulars. The footnote defines 'shari'a' and 'naw' (species), the latter explaining the logical term.

Philosophers on Revelation: Insufficient for Faith
The author describes the philosophers' view on revelation and states its insufficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation of revelation as an emanation from divine providence and why it is considered insufficient for the necessary truths of religion.

Core Idea
The philosophers' view of revelation lacks divine intent and purpose, falling short of religious necessity.

The author presents the philosophers' account of revelation as a natural emanation from God's prior knowledge, devoid of will and purpose. After laying out their argument from divine providence, he delivers a sharp critique: this rational explanation does not capture what is known with certainty from religion. This passage sets the stage for understanding the Islamic critique of philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the philosophers' description of revelation and the author's response.
Notice the technical terms like 'divine providence' and 'necessary religion'.
2
Identify the philosophers' view
Identify how the philosophers define revelation: as specialized forms and speech occurring in the prophet's hearing, and their explanation that it emanates from divine providence.
3
Locate the critique
Find the final sentence where the author states that this view does not suffice for what is known necessarily from religion.
This is the key point of the lesson.
4
Reflect on the insufficiency
Consider what the philosophers' account misses: divine intent, purpose, and a personal God who communicates willfully.
This connects to the Islamic creedal understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختصاص بعالم العناصر أشباحاً مصورة تخاطبه، وتحدث في سمعه كلاماً منظوماً يحفظ ويتلى، وهذا هو الوحي، ونزول الملك والكتاب، وأما كون ذلك من الله تعالى لنظام المعاش، ونجاة المعاد، وصلاح العباد مع نفي القصد والغرض من أفعاله، والعلم بالجزئي على الوجه الجزئي في أوصافه، فقرروه بأن العناية الإلهية، أعني إحاطة علمه السابق بنظام الموجودات على الوجه اللائق تقتضي فيضان ذلك النظام على الترتيب والتفصيل الذي من جملته وجود الشروع والشارع ليكون الموجود على وفق المعلوم، ولا خفاء في أن هذا لا يكفي فيما ثبت بالضرورة من الدين.
Specialization [occurring] in the world of elements [in the form of] portrayed semblances addressing him, and [there] occur in his hearing an ordered speech that is memorized and recited, and this is revelation (al-waḥy), and the descent of the angel and the book. As for that being from Allah the Exalted for the order of livelihood, the salvation of the afterlife, and the righteousness of servants, while negating intent (al-qaṣd) and purpose (al-gharaḍ) from His actions, and knowledge of the particular (al-juzʾī) in a particular manner in His attributes, they argued [for it] by saying that the divine providence (al-ʿināya al-ilāhiyya) — meaning His prior knowledge encompassing the system of existents in the appropriate way — necessitates the emanation of that system in the order and detail which includes [among its parts] the existence of the legislation (al-sharʿ) and the legislator (al-shāriʿ) so that the existent may be in accordance with the known. And it is no secret that this does not suffice for what is known by necessity (ḍarūra) from religion.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage explains the philosophers' concept of revelation and then criticizes it. It begins by describing revelation as the occurrence of portrayed semblances and ordered speech in the prophet's hearing. Then it states the philosophers' justification: that this revelation is from God for worldly order, salvation in the afterlife, and human righteousness, despite their denial of divine intent and purpose, and their denial of God's knowledge of particulars in a particular way. They argue that divine providence — God's prior knowledge of the best system — necessitates the emanation of that system, including the existence of a revealed law and a legislator, so that reality matches God's knowledge. The author then concludes that this explanation does not suffice for what is known necessarily from religion, implying that the Islamic understanding of revelation is far richer and not reducible to a mere emanation of a predetermined system.

Key Terms
وحي (revelation)
عناية إلهية (divine providence)
ضرورة الدين (necessity of religion)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' view of revelation as an emanation from divine providence is sufficient to explain religious revelation.
Correction: The author shows that this explanation lacks divine intent and purpose, and does not suffice for what is known necessarily from religion.

Checkpoint

C) The need for social order

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' rationalist explanation of revelation, based solely on divine providence, is insufficient to account for the necessary truths of religion, which affirm divine will, purpose, and personal communication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How would your worship change if you fully believed every command is infused with divine intent?
?
The author says the philosophers' view does not suffice for what is known by necessity from religion. If your faith were tested, would the emanation model satisfy your soul, or do you need a God who speaks to you with care and purpose?

The Philosophers' Rationale for Prophethood and Miracles
The author explains the philosophers' argument for the necessity of a prophet and miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument philosophers give for the necessity of a prophet, a divine law, and miracles as signs of prophethood.

Core Idea
Human social nature logically requires a prophet and miracles for a just society.

This passage presents the philosophers' rational case for why a prophet and divine law are necessary for human society. While the author notes that their explanation deviates from orthodox Islamic beliefs, understanding their reasoning provides insight into the natural theology approach to prophethood. Let's examine their argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow, then read the translation. Notice the logical chain from human nature to the need for a miracle.
Pay attention to how each step builds on the previous one.
2
Identify the starting premise
The philosophers begin with the observation that humans are social by nature and cannot survive alone. This cooperation requires rules and a lawgiver.
3
Trace the need for a lawgiver
Humans need a just law to regulate transactions. The lawgiver must have divine authority to be accepted. This special quality is prophethood.
4
Understand the role of miracles
The final step: the prophet must have a miracle as proof that his law is from God. This miracle compels belief and submission.
The philosophers define miracle as a special sign (amr mukhtaṣṣ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في طريقة الفلاسفة بالاحتياج إلى النبي والشريعة، وبثبوت المعجزة، لكن يقررون ذلك على وجه لا يوافق ما علم بالضرورة من الدين . أما تقريرهم في الاحتياج الى النبي فهو أن الإنسان مدني بالطبع ، أي محتاج في تعيشه إلى التمدن وهو اجتماعه مع بني نوعه للتعاون والتشارك في تحصيل ما يحتاجون إليه من الغذاء الموافق واللباس الواقي من الحر والبرد والمسكن الملائم بحسب الفصول المختلفة، والسلاح الحامي عن السباع والأعداء، فإن كل ذلك مما يحصل بالصناعات، ولا يمكن للإنسان الواحد القيام بجميعها، بل لا بد أن يخبز هذا لذلك، وذلك يخيط لآخر ، وآخر يتخذ الإبرة له إلى غير ذلك المصالح التي لا بقاء للنوع بدونها ، ثم ذلك التعاون والتشارك لا يتم إلا بمعاملات فيما بينهم ومعاوضات، ولا ينتظم إلا بقانون متفق عليه مبني على العدل والإنصاف، ضابط لما لا حصر له من الجزئيات، لئلا يقع الجور ، فيختل أمر النظام لما جبل عليه كل أحد من أنه يشتهي ما يحتاج إليه، ويغضب على من من يزاحمه . وذلك القانون هو الشرع، ولا بد له من شارع يقرره على ما ينبغي متميزاً عن الآخرين بخصوصية فيه من قبل خالق الكل واستحقاق طاعة وانقياد، وإلا لما قبلوه ، ولم ينقادوا له، وأن يكون إنساناً يخاطبهم ويلزمهم المعاملة على وفق ذلك القانون ويراجعونه في مواضع الاحتياج ومظان الاشتباه، فتلك الخصوصية البعثة والنبوة، وذلك الإنسان الشارع لقوانين المعاملات فيما بينهم، والسياسات في حق من يخرج من مصالح البقاء هو النبي، فلا بد من أمر مختص يدل على أن شريعته من عند ربه، ويقتضي لمن وقف عليه أن يقر بنبوته، وينقاد له وهو المعجزة.
Concerning the method of the philosophers regarding the need for a prophet and the law (al-sharīʿa), and the establishment of the miracle (al-muʿjiza), but they explain it in a way that does not agree with what is known by necessity from religion. As for their explanation regarding the need for a prophet, it is that man is social (madaniyy) by nature, i.e., he is in need in his livelihood of social organization (al-tamaddun), which is his association with his fellow humans for cooperation and participation in obtaining what they need of suitable food, clothing protective from heat and cold, appropriate housing according to different seasons, and weapons protecting from predators and enemies. For all of that is obtained through crafts, and it is not possible for one individual to undertake all of them. Rather, this one must bake for that one, that one sew for another, and another make the needle for him, and so on for other interests without which the species cannot survive. Then that cooperation and participation is not completed except through transactions among them and exchanges, and it is not organized except by an agreed-upon law based on justice and fairness, governing the countless particulars, lest injustice occur and the order be disrupted, due to what each person is naturally disposed to: he desires what he needs and becomes angry at whoever competes with him. That law is the sharīʿa (divine law), and it must have a lawgiver who establishes it as it should be, distinguished from others by a special quality (khuṣūṣiyya) from the Creator of all, deserving obedience and submission; otherwise they would not accept him nor submit to him. And that lawgiver must be a human being who addresses them and obliges them to act according to that law, and they refer to him in times of need and in cases of doubt. That special quality is prophethood (al-nubuwwa), and that human being who legislates the laws of transactions among them and the policies regarding those who deviate from the interests of survival is the prophet (al-nabiyy). Therefore, there must be a special sign (amr mukhtaṣṣ) indicating that his law is from his Lord, and necessitating that whoever witnesses it acknowledges his prophethood and submits to him; that is the miracle (al-muʿjiza).
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument for the necessity of prophets and miracles. It begins by noting that the philosophers' explanation does not align with what is known necessarily from religion, but it explains the rational basis of their view. The argument proceeds: humans are social by nature, needing cooperation for basic necessities like food, clothing, housing, and weapons. Since no individual can produce all these alone, collaboration is required. This collaboration requires transactions and exchanges, which need a just and fair law to regulate them and prevent injustice, given human tendencies to desire and compete. This law is the divine law (sharīʿa). Such a law requires a legislator (shāriʿ) who is distinguished by a special quality from God, deserving obedience, otherwise people would not accept him. The legislator must be a human being who can communicate and enforce the law. That special quality is prophethood, and that human is the prophet. Finally, the prophet must have a special sign proving his law is from God: that sign is a miracle.

Key Terms
مدني بالطبع (social by nature)
شريعة (divine law)
شارع (legislator)
معجزة (miracle)
نبوة (prophethood)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think the philosophers deny the need for prophets entirely.
Correction: This passage shows that philosophers actually argue that a prophet and divine law are rationally necessary, though their reasoning differs from Islamic orthodoxy.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why is a lawgiver with a special divine quality necessary?
A) Because humans cannot agree on any law.
B) Because people would not accept a lawgiver without divine authority.
C) Because natural law is insufficient.
D) Because only a prophet can perform miracles.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that the necessity of a prophet and miracle is rationally deducible from human social nature and the need for a just society.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in daily transactions (buying, selling, agreements), do you consciously measure your actions against the prophet's law (sharīʿa) or primarily against man-made standards of fairness? How does this reflect your true belief in prophethood?
?
If you saw a miracle today that confirmed a prophet's message, would it instantly transform your obedience? Or have you already been given enough signs? What is holding you back from complete submission to the prophet's law?
?
The philosophers argue that a just society is impossible without a prophet. Does your life reflect a deep reliance on the prophetic guidance, or do you secretly trust human wisdom more? Where is your ultimate hope for justice?

The Three Special Qualities of a Prophet According to Philosophers
The author details the philosophers' three special qualities of a prophet and their psychic explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three special qualities of a prophet according to the philosophers and the psychic powers that enable them, as well as the role of the challenge (taḥaddī) in making them true distinctive signs.

Core Idea
Prophets possess three powers: holy soul, imagination, and motive power, enabling knowledge, angelic vision, and miracles.

This passage from al-Maqāṣid explains what philosophers consider the three distinctive features of a prophet: knowledge of the unseen, miracles, and seeing angels with hearing their speech. It then unpacks each quality in terms of the prophet's psychic faculties—holy soul, imaginative power, and motive power—and defends these against objections by invoking the condition of a challenge (taḥaddī).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the three qualities listed at the beginning.
2
Identify the three special qualities
List the three qualities: knowledge of the unseen, miracles, and seeing the angel with hearing his speech.
These are the distinctive marks of prophethood according to the philosophers.
3
Understand the psychic explanation for each quality
The text explains each quality through a specific psychic power: holy soul for knowledge, imaginative power for angelic vision, motive power for miracles.
Focus on how each power operates and why it is unique to prophets.
4
Analyze the refutation of objections
The passage addresses the objection that these qualities can appear in non-prophets. Note the role of the challenge (taḥaddī) in making them true special qualities.
The challenge transforms relative specialty into absolute.
5
Reflect on the role of taḥaddī
Consider why the challenge is necessary and how it distinguishes prophetic miracles from other extraordinary events.
This is a key concept in Islamic theology regarding miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا : وهذا الإنسان هو الذي يجتمع فيه خواص ثلاث: هي الاطلاع على المغيبات، وظهور خوارق العادات ومشاهدة الملك مع سماع كلامه. ومعنى ذلك على ما شرحه في الشفاء وغيره أنه يكون كاملاً في قوته النفسانية، أعني الإنسانية والحيوانية المدركة والمحركة ، بمعنى أن نفسه القدسية بصفاء جوهرها، وشدة اتصالها بالمباديء العالية المنتقشة بصور الكائنات ماضيها وحاضرها، وآتيها، وقلة التفاتها إلى الأمور الجاذبة إلى الخسة السافلة، تكون بحيث يحصل لها جميع ما يمكن للنوع ،دفعه، أو قريباً من دفعه، إذ لا بخل هناك ولا احتجاب، وإنما المانع هو انجذاب القوابل إلى عالم الطبيعة، وانغماسها في الشواغل عن عالم العقل، وأن قوته المتخيلة تكون بحيث يتمثل لها العقول المجردة صوراً وأشباحاً يخاطبونه ويسمعونه كلاماً منظوماً محفوظاً، وأن قوته المحركة تكون بحيث يطيع لها هيولي العناصر، فيتصرف فيها تصرفها في بدنه، فيعنون بالخصائص هذه القوى، وبمشاهدة الملك هذا المعنى، فلا يرد الاعتراض بأن الاطلاع على المغيبات وظهور خوارق العادات قد يوجد لغير الأنبياء، فلا يكون من خواصهم، وأن مشاهدة الملك وسماع كلامه مجرد عبارة لا يقولون بمعناها على أن الخاصة قد تطلق على الإضافية، وأن ما ذكر بمجرد اعتبار مقارنته بالتحدي يصير خاصة حقيقية .
They said: This human being is one in whom three special qualities (khuṣūṣ) come together: knowledge of the unseen (al-ittilāʿ ʿalā al-mughayyabāt), occurrence of miraculous acts (khawāriq al-ʿādāt), and seeing the angel (mushāhadat al-malak) with hearing his speech. And the meaning of that, as explained in al-Shifāʾ and elsewhere, is that he is complete in his psychic power (qūwa nafsāniyya), i.e., the human and animal powers of perception and motion, meaning that his holy soul (nafs qudsiyya), due to the purity of its essence, its strong connection to the high principles that are imprinted with the forms of beings, past, present, and future, and its little attention to matters that attract to base lowliness, is such that all that is possible for the species to obtain is attained by it, or close to it, since there is no stinginess or veiling there; rather, the only obstacle is the attraction of the recipients to the natural world and their immersion in distractions from the intellectual world. And that his imaginative power (al-qūwa al-mutakhayyila) is such that the abstract intellects appear to it as forms and images that address him and he hears from them ordered preserved speech. And that his motive power (al-qūwa al-muḥarrika) is such that the matter of the elements obeys it, so it acts in them as it acts in his body. Thus, they mean by the [special] qualities these powers, and by seeing the angel this meaning. Hence, the objection is not valid that knowledge of the unseen and occurrence of miracles may be found in non-prophets, so they are not among their special qualities, and that seeing the angel and hearing his speech is merely an expression whose meaning they do not affirm, although the special quality (al-khāṣṣa) may be used in a relative sense, and that what is mentioned, by merely considering its conjunction with the challenge (al-taḥaddī), becomes a true special quality.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage explains the three special qualities of a prophet according to the philosophers: knowledge of the unseen, miracles, and seeing angels with hearing their speech. It then provides the philosophical explanation of these qualities using the prophet's psychic powers. The holy soul (nafs qudsiyya), due to its purity and connection to higher principles, acquires all possible knowledge of beings past, present, and future. The imaginative power (qūwa mutakhayyila) enables the prophet to see abstract intellects as angels and hear their speech. The motive power (qūwa muḥarrika) allows control over the elemental matter, producing miracles. Finally, the passage refutes potential objections: these qualities might be found in non-prophets, but when combined with a challenge (taḥaddī) to produce something impossible, they become true distinctive signs of prophethood. The philosophers also note that 'specialty' can be relative, but with the challenge it becomes real.

خواص ثلاث
قوة نفسانية
نفس قدسية
قوة متخيلة
قوة محركة
تحدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that miracles alone are sufficient to prove prophethood, without considering the psychic framework or the condition of challenge.
Correction: The philosophers argue that miracles must be understood as acts of the motive power, and that true prophetic signs require a challenge (taḥaddī) that cannot be matched.

Checkpoint

A) The holy soul (nafs qudsiyya)
B) The imaginative power (qūwa mutakhayyila)
C) The motive power (qūwa muḥarrika)
D) The rational power (qūwa ʿāqila)

Key Takeaway
The philosophers explain prophecy through three psychic powers (holy soul, imagination, motive power) that give prophets knowledge of the unseen, vision of angels, and miracles, and these become true signs when accompanied by a challenge (taḥaddī).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says the only obstacle to attaining knowledge is 'attraction to the natural world and immersion in distractions.' What specific distraction in your life right now is most preventing you from drawing closer to Allah? What will you do about it today?
?
The prophet's holy soul is described as 'having little attention to matters that attract to base lowliness.' Name one base lowliness you cling to. What would it cost you to turn away from it, and are you willing to pay that price?
?
The challenge (taḥaddī) is what transforms an ordinary quality into a prophetic sign. What test from Allah are you currently avoiding? If you faced it with full trust, what might He reveal to you about yourself?

The Necessity of a Prophet and the Miracle as Proof
Establishing the need for a prophet and his miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine law requires a lawgiver (prophet) and why a miracle is necessary to verify his prophethood.

Core Idea
A prophet must be backed by a miracle to prove his divine mandate.

In this passage, the author establishes the logical necessity of a prophet for divine law and the need for a distinguishing sign (the miracle) that compels recognition. This foundation is essential for understanding prophethood in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow.
Notice how the author builds from law to lawgiver to prophet to miracle.
2
Identify the Argument Structure
The author states that divine law requires a lawgiver who must be distinguished by a particularity from God. This particularity is prophethood, and the lawgiver is the prophet. Then he concludes that a miracle is needed to prove the divine origin.
3
Reflect on the Role of the Miracle
Ask yourself: why is a miracle necessary? According to the passage, it is the 'specific matter' that indicates the law is from God and compels acknowledgment.
Consider what would happen without a miracle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يزاحمه . وذلك القانون هو الشرع، ولا بد له من شارع يقرره على ما ينبغي متميزاً عن الآخرين بخصوصية فيه من قبل خالق الكل واستحقاق طاعة وانقياد، وإلا لما قبلوه ، ولم ينقادوا له، وأن يكون إنساناً يخاطبهم ويلزمهم المعاملة على وفق ذلك القانون ويراجعونه في مواضع الاحتياج ومظان الاشتباه، فتلك الخصوصية البعثة والنبوة، وذلك الإنسان الشارع لقوانين المعاملات فيما بينهم، والسياسات في حق من يخرج من مصالح البقاء هو النبي، فلا بد من أمر مختص يدل على أن شريعته من عند ربه، ويقتضي لمن وقف عليه أن يقر بنبوته، وينقاد له وهو المعجزة.

قالوا : وهذا الإنسان هو الذي يجتمع فيه خواص ثلاث: هي الاطلاع على المغيبات، وظهور خوارق العادات ومشاهدة الملك مع سماع كلامه. ومعنى ذلك على ما شرحه في الشفاء وغيره أنه يكون كاملاً في قوته النفسانية، أعني الإنسانية والحيوانية المدركة والمحركة ، بمعنى أن نفسه القدسية بصفاء جوهرها، وشدة اتصالها بالمباديء العالية المنتقشة بصور الكائنات ماضيها وحاضرها، وآتيها، وقلة التفاتها إلى الأمور الجاذبة إلى الخسة السافلة، تكون بحيث يحصل لها جميع ما يمكن للنوع ،دفعه، أو قريباً من دفعه، إذ لا بخل هناك ولا احتجاب، وإنما المانع هو انجذاب القوابل إلى عالم الطبيعة، وانغماسها في الشواغل عن عالم العقل، وأن قوته المتخيلة تكون بحيث يتمثل لها العقول المجردة صوراً وأشباحاً يخاطبونه ويسمعونه كلاماً منظوماً محفوظاً، وأن قوته المحركة تكون بحيث يطيع لها هيولي العناصر، فيتصرف فيها تصرفها في بدنه، فيعنون بالخصائص هذه القوى، وبمشاهدة الملك هذا المعنى، فلا يرد الاعتراض بأن الاطلاع على المغيبات وظهور خوارق العادات قد يوجد لغير الأنبياء، فلا يكون من خواصهم، وأن مشاهدة الملك وسماع كلامه مجرد عبارة لا يقولون بمعناها على أن الخاصة (۱) قد تطلق على الإضافية، وأن ما ذكر بمجرد اعتبار مقارنته بالتحدي يصير خاصة حقيقية .

(۱) الخاصة : كلية مقولة على أفراد حقيقية واحدة فقط قولاً عرضياً سواء وجد في جميع أفراده كالكاتب بالقوة بالنسبة إلى الإنسان أو في بعض أفراده كالكاتب بالفعل بالنسبة إليه فالكلية مستدركة وقولنا فقط يخرج الجنس، والعرض العام لأنهما مقولان على حقائق وقولنا قولاً عرضياً يخرج النوع والفصل لآت قولهما على ما تحتهما ذاتي لا عرضي، وخاصة الشيء ما لا يوجد بدون الشيء والشيء قد يوجد بدونها مثل الألف واللام لا يوجدان بدون الاسم ، والاسم يوجد بدونهما كما في زيد.
It conflicts with him. And that law is the Shari'a, and it must have a legislator who establishes it as it ought to be, distinguished from others by a particularity in him from the Creator of all and deserving of obedience and submission, otherwise they would not accept him nor submit to him. And that he be a human being who addresses them and obliges them to deal according to that law, and they refer to him in times of need and places of doubt. That particularity is the mission and prophethood, and that human being who legislates laws of transactions among them and policies regarding those who deviate from the interests of survival is the prophet. Therefore, there must be a specific matter indicating that his law is from his Lord, and requiring the one who witnesses it to acknowledge his prophethood and submit to him — and that is the miracle. They said: This human being is one in whom three characteristics are combined: knowledge of unseen things, the occurrence of extraordinary habits, and seeing the angel along with hearing his speech. And its meaning according to what is explained in al-Shifā' and other works is that he is perfect in his psychic powers, namely the human and animal powers of perception and motion. That is, his holy soul, due to the purity of its substance and its strong connection to the high principles which are impressed with the forms of all beings — past, present, and future — and its lack of attention to matters that attract to lowliness and baseness, becomes such that it attains all that the species can attain, or close to it, for there is no stinginess or veiling there, the only obstacle being the attraction of receptacles to the world of nature and their immersion in distractions from the world of intellect. And that his imaginative power becomes such that the abstract intellects appear to him as forms and likenesses that address him and make him hear well-ordered preserved speech. And that his motive power becomes such that the matter of the elements obeys him, and he acts upon them as they act upon his body. So by 'characteristics' they mean these powers, and by 'seeing the angel' they mean this meaning. Thus the objection does not arise that knowledge of unseen things and the occurrence of extraordinary habits may be found in non-prophets, and thus are not among their distinctive characteristics, and that seeing the angel and hearing his speech is a mere expression whose meaning they do not affirm, given that 'characteristic' (al-khāṣṣa) may be used relatively. And what is mentioned, simply by considering its conjunction with the challenge (al-taḥaddī), becomes a true characteristic. (Footnote) al-khāṣṣa: a universal predicated of only one true species accidentally, whether found in all its individuals, like 'potentially writer' with respect to man, or in some of its individuals, like 'actually writer' with respect to him. So the universality is taken into account, and our statement 'only' excludes the genus and the common accident because they are predicated of [multiple] realities; and our statement 'accidentally' excludes the species and the differentia because their predication of what is under them is essential, not accidental. And the characteristic of a thing is that without which the thing does not exist, while the thing may exist without it, like the definite article 'al' and 'lam' do not exist without the noun, and the noun exists without them, as in 'Zayd'.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and argument-demonstrating the necessity of a prophet and the distinguishing sign (mu'jiza) that establishes his prophethood. The author first states that divine law (shar') requires a lawgiver (shāri') who is distinguished by a particularity from the Creator, deserving of obedience. This particularity is prophethood, and the lawgiver is the prophet. To prove that his law is from God, a specific sign is needed—the miracle (al-mu'jiza). Then, the author reports that three characteristics combine in a prophet: knowledge of the unseen, miracles (khawāriq al-ʿādāt), and seeing the angel with hearing his speech. He explains these through a psychological framework from al-Shifā': the prophet's holy soul is connected to high principles (the intellects and celestial souls), so it can know all events; his imaginative power enables him to perceive abstract intellects as forms and speech; his motive power controls elemental matter. This explanation preempts an objection that non-prophets may also possess such powers. The author responds by noting that 'characteristic' (al-khāṣṣa) can be relative, but when combined with a challenge (al-taḥaddī), it becomes a true distinctive characteristic. The footnote clarifies the technical definition of 'al-khāṣṣa' as a logical term.

شارع
نبوة
معجزة

Correction: The author explicitly states that a specific miracle is necessary ('لا بد من أمر مختص') to indicate that the law is from God and to compel acknowledgment of prophethood.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Three Characteristics of a Prophet and Their Psychological Basis
Explaining the three prophetic characteristics and the role of challenge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinctive characteristics of a prophet and how they are explained through the powers of the soul, and why they are exclusive when combined with a challenge.

Core Idea
A prophet combines knowledge of the unseen, miracles, and angelic encounter, grounded in perfected soul-powers.

This passage delves into the specific traits that mark a true prophet: knowledge of the unseen, supernatural acts, and seeing/hearing the angel. The author provides a psychological explanation from Avicenna's framework, detailing perfection in the holy soul, imaginative power, and motive power. He then addresses the objection that non-prophets may possess similar abilities, resolving it by introducing the crucial element of challenge (tahaddi) that makes a miracle distinctive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Characteristics
List the three characteristics the author says combine in a prophet. Notice each one corresponds to a specific power of the soul.
2
Understand the Psychological Explanation
The author explains how the holy soul, imaginative power, and motive power are perfected to produce knowledge of the unseen, angelic encounter, and miracles.
This is based on Avicenna's psychology from 'al-Shifā'.
3
Examine the Objection and Response
The objection is that non-prophets may also have these abilities. The author responds by highlighting the role of challenge (tahaddi) in making a miracle a true characteristic.
4
Review the Footnote on 'Khassa'
The footnote clarifies the logical definition of 'characteristic' (al-khāṣṣa) as a universal predicated of only one reality accidentally.
This helps understand how a miracle can be exclusive when paired with challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يزاحمه . وذلك القانون هو الشرع، ولا بد له من شارع يقرره على ما ينبغي متميزاً عن الآخرين بخصوصية فيه من قبل خالق الكل واستحقاق طاعة وانقياد، وإلا لما قبلوه ، ولم ينقادوا له، وأن يكون إنساناً يخاطبهم ويلزمهم المعاملة على وفق ذلك القانون ويراجعونه في مواضع الاحتياج ومظان الاشتباه، فتلك الخصوصية البعثة والنبوة، وذلك الإنسان الشارع لقوانين المعاملات فيما بينهم، والسياسات في حق من يخرج من مصالح البقاء هو النبي، فلا بد من أمر مختص يدل على أن شريعته من عند ربه، ويقتضي لمن وقف عليه أن يقر بنبوته، وينقاد له وهو المعجزة.

قالوا : وهذا الإنسان هو الذي يجتمع فيه خواص ثلاث: هي الاطلاع على المغيبات، وظهور خوارق العادات ومشاهدة الملك مع سماع كلامه. ومعنى ذلك على ما شرحه في الشفاء وغيره أنه يكون كاملاً في قوته النفسانية، أعني الإنسانية والحيوانية المدركة والمحركة ، بمعنى أن نفسه القدسية بصفاء جوهرها، وشدة اتصالها بالمباديء العالية المنتقشة بصور الكائنات ماضيها وحاضرها، وآتيها، وقلة التفاتها إلى الأمور الجاذبة إلى الخسة السافلة، تكون بحيث يحصل لها جميع ما يمكن للنوع ،دفعه، أو قريباً من دفعه، إذ لا بخل هناك ولا احتجاب، وإنما المانع هو انجذاب القوابل إلى عالم الطبيعة، وانغماسها في الشواغل عن عالم العقل، وأن قوته المتخيلة تكون بحيث يتمثل لها العقول المجردة صوراً وأشباحاً يخاطبونه ويسمعونه كلاماً منظوماً محفوظاً، وأن قوته المحركة تكون بحيث يطيع لها هيولي العناصر، فيتصرف فيها تصرفها في بدنه، فيعنون بالخصائص هذه القوى، وبمشاهدة الملك هذا المعنى، فلا يرد الاعتراض بأن الاطلاع على المغيبات وظهور خوارق العادات قد يوجد لغير الأنبياء، فلا يكون من خواصهم، وأن مشاهدة الملك وسماع كلامه مجرد عبارة لا يقولون بمعناها على أن الخاصة (۱) قد تطلق على الإضافية، وأن ما ذكر بمجرد اعتبار مقارنته بالتحدي يصير خاصة حقيقية .

(۱) الخاصة : كلية مقولة على أفراد حقيقية واحدة فقط قولاً عرضياً سواء وجد في جميع أفراده كالكاتب بالقوة بالنسبة إلى الإنسان أو في بعض أفراده كالكاتب بالفعل بالنسبة إليه فالكلية مستدركة وقولنا فقط يخرج الجنس، والعرض العام لأنهما مقولان على حقائق وقولنا قولاً عرضياً يخرج النوع والفصل لآت قولهما على ما تحتهما ذاتي لا عرضي، وخاصة الشيء ما لا يوجد بدون الشيء والشيء قد يوجد بدونها مثل الألف واللام لا يوجدان بدون الاسم ، والاسم يوجد بدونهما كما في زيد.
It conflicts with him. And that law is the Shari'a, and it must have a legislator who establishes it as it ought to be, distinguished from others by a particularity in him from the Creator of all and deserving of obedience and submission, otherwise they would not accept him nor submit to him. And that he be a human being who addresses them and obliges them to deal according to that law, and they refer to him in times of need and places of doubt. That particularity is the mission and prophethood, and that human being who legislates laws of transactions among them and policies regarding those who deviate from the interests of survival is the prophet. Therefore, there must be a specific matter indicating that his law is from his Lord, and requiring the one who witnesses it to acknowledge his prophethood and submit to him — and that is the miracle. They said: This human being is one in whom three characteristics are combined: knowledge of unseen things, the occurrence of extraordinary habits, and seeing the angel along with hearing his speech. And its meaning according to what is explained in al-Shifā' and other works is that he is perfect in his psychic powers, namely the human and animal powers of perception and motion. That is, his holy soul, due to the purity of its substance and its strong connection to the high principles which are impressed with the forms of all beings — past, present, and future — and its lack of attention to matters that attract to lowliness and baseness, becomes such that it attains all that the species can attain, or close to it, for there is no stinginess or veiling there, the only obstacle being the attraction of receptacles to the world of nature and their immersion in distractions from the world of intellect. And that his imaginative power becomes such that the abstract intellects appear to him as forms and likenesses that address him and make him hear well-ordered preserved speech. And that his motive power becomes such that the matter of the elements obeys him, and he acts upon them as they act upon his body. So by 'characteristics' they mean these powers, and by 'seeing the angel' they mean this meaning. Thus the objection does not arise that knowledge of unseen things and the occurrence of extraordinary habits may be found in non-prophets, and thus are not among their distinctive characteristics, and that seeing the angel and hearing his speech is a mere expression whose meaning they do not affirm, given that 'characteristic' (al-khāṣṣa) may be used relatively. And what is mentioned, simply by considering its conjunction with the challenge (al-taḥaddī), becomes a true characteristic. (Footnote) al-khāṣṣa: a universal predicated of only one true species accidentally, whether found in all its individuals, like 'potentially writer' with respect to man, or in some of its individuals, like 'actually writer' with respect to him. So the universality is taken into account, and our statement 'only' excludes the genus and the common accident because they are predicated of [multiple] realities; and our statement 'accidentally' excludes the species and the differentia because their predication of what is under them is essential, not accidental. And the characteristic of a thing is that without which the thing does not exist, while the thing may exist without it, like the definite article 'al' and 'lam' do not exist without the noun, and the noun exists without them, as in 'Zayd'.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and argument-demonstrating the necessity of a prophet and the distinguishing sign (mu'jiza) that establishes his prophethood. The author first states that divine law (shar') requires a lawgiver (shāri') who is distinguished by a particularity from the Creator, deserving of obedience. This particularity is prophethood, and the lawgiver is the prophet. To prove that his law is from God, a specific sign is needed—the miracle (al-mu'jiza). Then, the author reports that three characteristics combine in a prophet: knowledge of the unseen, miracles (khawāriq al-ʿādāt), and seeing the angel with hearing his speech. He explains these through a psychological framework from al-Shifā': the prophet's holy soul is connected to high principles (the intellects and celestial souls), so it can know all events; his imaginative power enables him to perceive abstract intellects as forms and speech; his motive power controls elemental matter. This explanation preempts an objection that non-prophets may also possess such powers. The author responds by noting that 'characteristic' (al-khāṣṣa) can be relative, but when combined with a challenge (al-taḥaddī), it becomes a true distinctive characteristic. The footnote clarifies the technical definition of 'al-khāṣṣa' as a logical term.

خوارق العادات
الملك
خاصة
تحدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-prophets can have the same three characteristics as prophets, so they are not unique.
Correction: While individuals may possess some of these abilities, they are not true characteristics until combined with the challenge (tahaddi), which makes them exclusive to the prophet.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the objection that non-prophets can have knowledge of the unseen and miracles?
A) He denies that non-prophets can have them.
B) He argues these are only possible for prophets.
C) He says that when combined with the challenge (tahaddi), they become exclusive.
D) He says they are not real characteristics.

Key Takeaway
A prophet's distinctive qualities, when coupled with a challenge, become irreplicable signs of his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been impressed by someone's spiritual experiences or miraculous claims? Without a divine challenge, how can you be sure they are prophetic? Commit today to test any claim of prophethood against the criterion of an undeniable challenge.
?
The prophet's soul is described as 'pure' and 'strongly connected to higher principles' with 'lack of attention to matters that attract to lowliness and baseness'. What lowly attachments in your own soul distract you from the world of intellect? What one thing can you begin to detach from this week?
?
The footnote distinguishes between a relative characteristic (like 'writer') and a true essential one. In your own faith, are you relying on relative signs (feelings, coincidences) or on the definitive signs that God has sent through prophets with clear challenges? What would you need to treat these signs as truly authoritative in your life?

Acting on Other Bodies: The Elemental Power of Perfect Souls
Introduces the first extraordinary ability of perfect souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how perfect souls can act on other bodies and even the entire elemental world as if they were its soul.

Core Idea
The perfect soul can become like the soul of the world, acting on all elements.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid discusses the extraordinary abilities granted to perfect souls. The first ability mentioned is the power to influence bodies beyond their own, extending to the entire elemental realm. This lesson explores that remarkable claim and its implications for understanding miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text carefully. It is a single sentence describing the first ability.
Notice the phrase 'كأنها نفس لعالم العناصر' — a powerful metaphor.
2
Understand the claim
The claim is that a perfect soul can act on other bodies and even the whole world of elements, as if it were the soul of that world. Consider what this implies about the soul's power.
3
Connect to miracles
Relate this ability to the broader concept of miracles (karamat or mu'jizat). Think about how such power is a sign of divine favor.
This ability is distinct from the soul's own body control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من القوة بحيث يتصرف في أجسام أخر غير ،بدنها، بل في كلية العناصر، حتى كأنها نفس لعالم العناصر. (1) والثالث: الإمساك عن القوت مدة غير معتادة، وليس ببعيد كما في بعض الأمراض لاشتغال الطبيعة بهضم الأخلاط الفاسدة، وتحليل المواد الردية عن تحليل المواد المحمودة والرطوبات الأصلية المحوج إلى البدل، فيجوز في حق الأشخاص الكاملة لانجذاب نفوسهم إلى جناب القدس بالكلية، واستتباعها القوى الجسمانية التي بها الهضم والشهوة والتغذية وما يتعلق بذلك، بل لا يبعد أن يكون هذا من حق هؤلاء أولى وأقرب منه في المرض لكون احتياج المريض إلى الغذاء أوفر وأوفى أما أولاً فلتحلل رطوباته بسبب الحرارة الغريبة المسماة بسوء المزاج ) . وأما ثانياً فلفرط احتياجه إلى حفظ القوى البدنية بحفظ الرطوبات التي بها تعتدل الحرارة الغريزية، وذلك لما عرض لها بسبب المرض المضاد لها من الفتور. وأما ثالثاً فلاختصاص العارف بأمر يقتضي الاستغناء الغذاء، وهو السكون البدني الحاصل بسبب ترك القوى البدنية أفاعيلها عند متابعتها النفس.
From the power such that it acts on other bodies besides its own, rather on the entire elements, as if it were the soul of the world of elements. (1) And the third: abstaining from food for an unusual period, and it is not far-fetched, as in some diseases because nature is occupied with digesting corrupt humors and analyzing bad materials rather than analyzing praiseworthy materials and original moistures that require replacement. So it is permissible in the case of perfect persons due to the attraction of their souls entirely to the holy presence, and their subjugation of the bodily powers by which digestion, appetite, nutrition and related things occur. Rather, it is not unlikely that this is more fitting and closer for these [perfect persons] than in illness, because the patient's need for food is more abundant and sufficient. First, because of the decomposition of his moistures due to the strange heat called 'bad temperament'. Second, because of his extreme need to preserve his bodily powers by preserving the moistures by which the innate heat is moderated, due to the weakness that has befallen them because of the disease that is opposed to them. Third, because of the mystic's being distinguished by a matter that necessitates independence from food, which is the bodily stillness resulting from the bodily powers ceasing their actions when they follow the soul.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of two extraordinary abilities of perfect souls (like prophets or saints). The first, briefly mentioned, is the power to act on other bodies beyond their own, even on the entire elemental world, as if the soul becomes the soul of that world. The third ability is prolonged fasting, which is compared to medical conditions where the body is busy eliminating waste. For perfect persons, this is even more fitting than in illness because their souls are attracted to the divine, causing the bodily powers to cease their functions, including the need for food. The author gives three reasons why this is more appropriate for them than for the sick: (1) the sick have decomposition due to bad temperament, (2) the sick need to preserve moisture to moderate innate heat, (3) the mystic has bodily stillness due to the soul's dominance, making food unnecessary. The second ability is missing from the text due to OCR limitations.

Key Terms
قوة
أشخاص كاملة
عناصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the passage compare the perfect soul to?
A) The soul of the world
B) The angelic realm
C) The physical body
D) The divine essence

Key Takeaway
The perfect soul can extend its influence over the entire elemental world, acting as its soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe your soul has the capacity to influence the world beyond your own body? If so, how would your daily actions change if you lived as if your soul were the soul of your surroundings?
?
If a perfect soul becomes like the soul of the world, what is your soul currently attached to—your own desires or a greater reality? What concrete step can you take today to expand your soul's influence beyond yourself?
?
This passage describes a miraculous power that is real. Are you living as if you possess such a soul? Why or why not? What will you do today to acknowledge and exercise the power of your soul over your own actions, and perhaps over your immediate environment?

Prolonged Fasting: The Miracle of Independence from Food
Explains the third extraordinary ability: prolonged fasting, with a medical analogy and three reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why perfect souls can abstain from food for extended periods and why this is even more fitting for them than for the sick.

Core Idea
Perfect souls transcend the need for food because their bodily powers cease in pursuit of the divine.

The third extraordinary ability discussed is prolonged fasting. The author draws an analogy with certain diseases but argues it is even more appropriate for perfect souls. This lesson unpacks the three reasons given for this claim, illuminating the relationship between spiritual perfection and bodily needs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on fasting
Read the passage starting from 'والثالث: الإمساك عن القوت' to the end. Note the comparison to disease.
The passage contains three numbered reasons.
2
Understand the comparison to disease
The author says prolonged fasting is not far-fetched, just as some diseases temporarily suppress hunger. But learn how it differs for perfect souls.
In disease, nature is busy with corrupt humors; in perfect souls, the soul is drawn to the divine.
3
Analyze the three reasons
Identify and understand each of the three reasons why prolonged fasting is more fitting for perfect souls than for the sick. The reasons are given with 'أما أولاً' etc.
Focus on the third reason: bodily stillness resulting from the bodily powers ceasing when they follow the soul.
4
Reflect on the concept of bodily stillness
Consider what 'sukūn badani' (bodily stillness) means. It implies a temporary suspension of normal bodily functions due to spiritual absorption.

The Mechanism of Revelation and Angelic Appearance
Explains how revelation occurs through the prophetic imaginative faculty connecting to the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical account of how revelation occurs through the prophetic imaginative faculty connecting to the unseen realm.

Core Idea
Revelation is a prophetic connection to the unseen, mediated by a powerful imaginative faculty.

This passage presents the philosophers' explanation of how revelation and the appearance of angels occur, despite angels being abstract entities. It draws an analogy with dreams and explains the role of the prophet's strong soul and imagination. Read on to see how they reconcile the abstract nature of angels with their perceptible forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overview of the philosophers' argument.
Notice the dense technical language.
2
Understand the dream analogy
The passage starts by comparing revelation to dreams, where images and sounds are perceived without external reality. This sets the foundation.
The key faculties are the imaginative faculty and common sense.
3
Identify the prophet's special qualities
The passage describes the prophet as having a noble soul, strong connection to the intellect, and a powerful imagination that can receive from the unseen.
4
Trace the process of revelation
See how the prophet's imaginative faculty forms images of abstract intellects (angels) and hears speech, all from God and His angels.
The Active Intellect plays a special role.
5
Consider the highest level
The passage ends with the ultimate perfection: direct vision of God and hearing His speech without intermediary.
This is the peak of prophetic experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تقرير هم لنزول الوحي وظهور الملك مع أنه من المجردات (۳) دون الأجسام، فهو أن النائم ومن يجري مجراه في عدم استيلاء الحواس عليه قد يشاهد صوراً غريبة، ويسمع أصواتاً عجيبة ، وليست بمعدومة صرفة ولا موجودة في الخارج، بل في القوة المتخيلة والحس المشترك، وربما لا يكون متأدية إليه من طرق الحواس الظاهرة، بل من عالم آخر، فلا يبعد أن يكون لبعض أفراد الإنسان نفس شريفة شديدة الاتصال بعالم العقل قليلة الالتفات إلى عالم الحس ومتخيلة شديدة جداً قوية التلقي من عالم الغيب، قليلة الانغماس في جانب الظاهر، لا يعصيها المصورة ولا يشغلها المحسوسات عن أفعالها الخاصة، ويحصل لذلك الإنسان في اليقظة أن يتصل بعالم الغيب، ويتمثل لقوته المتخيلة العقول المجردة والنفوس السماوية أشباحاً مصورة سيما العقل الفعال) الذي له زيادة اختصاص بعالم العناصر، فتخاطبه وتحدث في سمعه كلاماً مسموعاً يحفظ ويتلي، ويكون ذلك من قبل الله وملائكته لا من الإنسان، وهذا معنى الوحي ونزول الملك والكتاب . وقد يكون ذلك على غاية الكمال، فيعبر عنها بمشاهدة وجه الله الكريم وسماع كلامه من غير واسطة .
As for their explanation of the descent of revelation and the appearance of the angel, despite it being from among the abstract entities (3) and not bodies, it is that the sleeper and those in his state, where the senses do not dominate him, may see strange forms and hear wondrous sounds, which are neither pure non-existence nor existing externally, but in the imaginative faculty and common sense. Perhaps they are not conveyed to him through the paths of the external senses, but from another world. So it is not unlikely that there exists for some individuals of mankind a noble soul strongly connected to the world of intellect, with little attention to the sensory world, and a very strong imagination, powerfully receptive from the world of the unseen, little immersed in the manifest aspect. The form-giving faculty does not disobey it, nor do perceptibles distract it from its own actions. So that person, while awake, may connect to the world of the unseen, and there appear to his imaginative faculty the abstract intellects and celestial souls as formed phantoms, especially the Active Intellect, which has a special connection to the world of elements. They address him and produce in his hearing audible speech that is memorized and recited. This is from God and His angels, not from the human. This is the meaning of revelation and the descent of the angel and the book. This may reach the utmost perfection, expressed as seeing the noble face of God and hearing His speech without intermediary.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the mechanism of revelation, according to the philosophers (as indicated by 'تقريرهم'). It argues that just as a sleeper can perceive strange images and sounds that are not external realities but exist in the imagination, so too a prophet, due to his strong connection to the intellect and his powerful imaginative faculty, can perceive the abstract intellects (like angels) as formed images and hear speech from them while awake. This is not from his own mind but from God and His angels. The highest level of this is the vision of God and hearing His speech directly, which is the ultimate perfection of revelation.

Key Terms
وحي
نزول الملك
قوة متخيلة
عقل فعال
مجردات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Revelation is merely a psychological product of the prophet's imagination.
Correction: The passage states that revelation is from God and His angels, using the imaginative faculty as a medium, not from the human.

Checkpoint
According to this explanation, where do the images and sounds of revelation exist?
A) In the external world
B) In the prophet's imaginative faculty
C) In the divine essence
D) In the common sense alone

Key Takeaway
Revelation is explained as the prophet's imaginative faculty receiving speech from abstract intellects, ultimately from God and His angels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage describes the prophet as having a soul 'strongly connected to the world of intellect' and 'little immersed in the manifest aspect.' In your own life, what distracts you most from the unseen world? What concrete step can you take today to immerse yourself less in sensory distractions and more in spiritual receptivity?
?
The passage explains that revelation uses the imaginative faculty but is ultimately from God. Have you ever dismissed an inner prompting as 'just your imagination' when it might have been divine guidance? How can you develop discernment to tell the difference, using the criteria this passage provides?
?
The prophet's imaginative faculty is described as 'not disobeyed by the form-giving faculty' and 'not distracted by perceptibles.' Is your own inner life obedient to your spiritual goals, or do your own thoughts and senses often lead you astray?
