Prophethood
intermediate
5 min
Divine Providence Necessitates Prophethood
Argues from divine providence to the necessity of prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers argued that God's comprehensive knowledge of the best order requires the existence of prophets and divine law.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of the best order includes prophets as necessary parts.

The philosophers faced a challenge: if God does not act for purposes or know particulars, how can they explain the sending of prophets? Their answer lies in the concept of divine providence (العناية الإلهية) — God's eternal knowledge of the most perfect system of existence. This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents that argument, drawing on Avicenna's Shifa to show that just as providence provides minor physical benefits, it must also provide the essential benefit of guidance through prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' position
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the description of what the philosophers deny (God's action by choice and knowledge of particulars) and what they affirm (divine providence).
This sets up the intellectual context: a non-theistic framework that still requires prophethood.
2
Identify the definition of divine providence
Find the definition of divine providence in the passage: it is God's preceding knowledge encompassing the system of existents in the best manner and at the appropriate times.
This definition is key to understanding the argument.
3
Follow the inference to the law and lawgiver
See how the passage concludes that this knowledge necessitates the existence of the law (الشرع) and the lawgiver (الشارع, i.e., the prophet), because they are part of the best order.
The argument does not rely on God's will, only on His knowledge.
4
Analyze the analogy from Avicenna
Consider the analogy: divine providence provides minor beneficial features like eyelashes and foot arches for survival, so it must also provide the essential benefit of prophecy for the survival of the human species.
This strengthens the argument by showing consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تقريرهم في كون النبي مبعوثاً من قبل الباري تعالى لحفظ النظام وصلاح العباد في المعاش والمعاد، مع أنهم لا يثبتون له الفعل بالاختيار والعلم بالجزئيات، ويقطعون بأنه - بل جميع المبادىء العالية ـ لا يفعل لغرض في الأمور السافلة، فهو أن العناية الإلهية بمخلوقاته، أعني إحاطة علمه السابق بنظام الموجودات على الوجه الأليق في الأوقات المترتبة التي يقع كل موجود منها في تلك الأوقات يقتضي إفاضة ذلك النظام على ذلك الترتيب والتفصيل الذي من جملته وجود الشرع والشارع ووجوب ما به يكون النظام على وجه الصواب، فيجب ذلك عنه وعن إحاطته بكيفية الصواب في ترتيب وجود الكل ليكون الموجود على وفق المعلوم وعلى أحسن النظام، وإن لم يكن هناك انبعاث قصد وطلب منه تعالى، وهذا ما قال في الشفاء إن العناية الإلهية تقتضي المصالح التي لها منفعة ما في البقاء، كإنبات الشعر على الأشفار وعلى الحاجبين، وتقعير الأخمص من القدمين فكيف لا تقتضي المنفعة التي هي في محل الضرورة للبقاء، ولتمهيد نظام الخير وأساس المنافع كلها، وكيف لا يجب وقد وجد ما هو مبني عليها ومتعلق بها ، وكيف يجوز أن يكون المبدأ الأول والملائكة بعده يعلمون ذلك ولا يعلمون هذا .
As for their explanation of the prophet being sent by the Exalted Creator to preserve order and the righteousness of servants in livelihood and afterlife, despite that they do not affirm for Him action by choice and knowledge of particulars, and they assert that He—indeed all the high principles—do not act for a purpose in lower matters, it is that divine providence for His creatures, that is, His preceding knowledge encompassing the system of existents in the most suitable manner in the ordered times in which each existent occurs, necessitates the emanation of that system according to that order and detail, which includes the existence of the law and the lawgiver and the necessity of that by which order is correctly established. So that is necessitated by Him and by His encompassing knowledge of the correct manner in ordering the existence of the whole, so that the existent conforms to the known and to the best order, even if there is no intention and request from Him. This is what he said in al-Shifa: 'Divine providence necessitates the beneficial interests that have some utility in survival, such as the growth of hair on the eyelids and eyebrows, and the arching of the soles of the feet. So how could it not necessitate the benefit that is in the place of necessity for survival, and for laying the foundation of the system of good and the basis of all benefits? And how is it not necessary, when that which is built upon it and related to it exists? And how is it permissible that the First Principle and the angels after Him know that but not know this?'
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of philosophers (أهل الفلسفة) that divine providence (العناية الإلهية) necessitates the sending of prophets. Although they deny that God acts for a purpose or knows particulars, they hold that God's knowledge of the best possible order entails the existence of everything that is part of that order, including the law (الشرع) and the lawgiver (الشارع, i.e., the prophet). The author quotes Avicenna's Shifa to draw an analogy: if divine providence provides minor benefits like eyelashes and arches in the feet for survival, how much more would it provide the essential benefit of prophecy, which is necessary for the survival of the human species and the establishment of the system of good? The argument concludes that it is impossible that the First Principle and the angels know these details but not the necessity of sending prophets.

Key Terms
عناية إلهية
نظام
شرع
شارع
مبدأ أول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the philosophers' argument requires that God wills or intends the sending of prophets.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the necessity arises from God's knowledge of the best order, not from any intention or purpose on His part.

Checkpoint
According to this philosophical argument, what is the basis for the necessity of sending a prophet?
A) God's mercy
B) God's punishment
C) Divine providence and knowledge
D) Human reason alone

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that God's perfect knowledge of the best possible order necessarily includes the sending of prophets, even without any intention on God's part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see God's provision in small, physical details like the hair on your eyelids, yet do you ignore the far greater provision of prophets sent to guide your soul? What specific teaching of the Prophet have you neglected, and what will you begin practicing today to treat it as a necessity for your salvation?
?
The philosophers argue that God's knowledge alone necessitates the best order without any intention. Does this challenge you to trust that God's plan for your life is perfect even when you cannot see His purpose? What trial are you facing right now that you need to surrender to that perfect knowledge?
?
If the preservation of order and righteousness depends on following the law brought by the prophet, how seriously do you take the Shari'ah in your daily decisions? Name one area where you have compromised and commit to realigning it this week.

Prophethood
intermediate
5 min
The Philosophers' Proof from Divine Providence
Philosophers' argument for prophethood from divine providence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers argue for the necessity of prophethood from the concept of divine providence, despite their denial of divine choice and particular knowledge.

Core Idea
Divine providence necessitates the existence of law and lawgiver for the best order.

The philosophers (falāsifa) affirm prophethood not from divine will but from necessitation. They argue that God's comprehensive prior knowledge (al-ʿināya) demands the best possible system, which includes a revealed law and a prophet. This lesson unpacks that argument and the famous analogy from Avicenna's al-Shifāʾ

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' challenge
The philosophers deny that God acts by choice or knows particulars. Yet they affirm prophethood. Note this tension.
They view God as a necessary cause, not a choosing agent.
2
Define divine providence
Divine providence is defined as God's encompassing prior knowledge of the best system of existents in their ordered times.
3
Trace the necessity chain
This knowledge necessitates the emanation of the system in reality, which includes the sharīʿa and the lawgiver.
The argument is causal: knowledge → system → law.
4
Examine the analogy from al-Shifāʾ
If providence provides lesser benefits like hair growth and foot arches, it must provide the greatest benefit: the law for survival and order.
This is an argument from lesser to greater.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
The conclusion: prophethood is necessary for the survival and perfection of humanity, even without divine intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تقريرهم في كون النبي مبعوثاً من قبل الباري تعالى لحفظ النظام وصلاح العباد في المعاش والمعاد، مع أنهم لا يثبتون له الفعل بالاختيار والعلم بالجزئيات، ويقطعون بأنه - بل جميع المبادىء العالية ـ لا يفعل لغرض في الأمور السافلة، فهو أن العناية الإلهية بمخلوقاته، أعني إحاطة علمه السابق بنظام الموجودات على الوجه الأليق في الأوقات المترتبة التي يقع كل موجود منها في تلك الأوقات يقتضي إفاضة ذلك النظام على ذلك الترتيب والتفصيل الذي من جملته وجود الشرع والشارع ووجوب ما به يكون النظام على وجه الصواب، فيجب ذلك عنه وعن إحاطته بكيفية الصواب في ترتيب وجود الكل ليكون الموجود على وفق المعلوم وعلى أحسن النظام، وإن لم يكن هناك انبعاث قصد وطلب منه تعالى، وهذا ما قال في الشفاء إن العناية الإلهية تقتضي المصالح التي لها منفعة ما في البقاء، كإنبات الشعر على الأشفار وعلى الحاجبين، وتقعير الأخمص من القدمين فكيف لا تقتضي المنفعة التي هي في محل الضرورة للبقاء، ولتمهيد نظام الخير وأساس المنافع كلها، وكيف لا يجب وقد وجد ما هو مبني عليها ومتعلق بها، وكيف يجوز أن يكون المبدأ الأول والملائكة بعده يعلمون ذلك ولا يعلمون هذا. ففي الجملة قالوا بوجوب البعثة ولزوم النبوة. فمن قال هي واجبة في الحكمة أراد تبقية النظام على الوجه اللائق، ومن قال في العناية أراد تمثل النظام في علمه الشامل. ومن قال في الطبيعة، أراد وجود النظام الكامل. ولقد أفصح عن المقصود بعض الإفصاح من قال: إن المدبر الذي يسوق النوع من النقصان إلى الكمال لا بد أن يبعث الأنبياء ويمهد الشرائع كما هو موجود في العالم، ليحصل النظام ويتعيش الأشخاص ويمكن لهم الوصول من النقصان إلى الكمال الذي خلقوا لأجله.
As for their demonstration that the prophet is sent from the Exalted Creator to preserve order and the well-being of servants in livelihood and the hereafter, despite the fact that they do not affirm for Him action by choice and knowledge of particulars, and they are certain that He—rather all high principles—does not act for a purpose in inferior matters, it is that divine providence (al-ʿināya al-ilāhiyya) for His creatures, by which I mean the encompassing of His prior knowledge of the system of existents in the most fitting manner in the ordered times in which each existent occurs in those times, necessitates the emanation of that system according to that order and detail, part of which is the existence of the Law (al-sharʿ) and the Lawgiver (al-shāriʿ) and the necessity of that by which order is correctly maintained. So that is necessary from Him and from His encompassing of the quality of correctness in arranging the existence of the whole so that the existent corresponds to what is known and is in the best order, even if there is no impulse of intention and request from Him. And this is what was said in al-Shifāʾ: 'Divine providence requires those benefits which have something of usefulness in survival, like growing hair on eyelids and eyebrows, and hollowing the arch of the foot. So how does it not require the benefit which is in the place of necessity for survival, and for establishing the system of good and the foundation of all benefits? How is it not necessary when what is built upon it and connected to it has already existed? How is it permissible that the First Principle and the angels after Him know that but not know this?' In short, they affirmed the obligation of sending prophets and the necessity of prophethood. For whoever said it is obligatory in wisdom (ḥikma) meant the preservation of order in a fitting manner; whoever said it is obligatory in providence (ʿināya) meant the manifestation of order in His comprehensive knowledge; whoever said it is obligatory in nature (ṭabīʿa) meant the existence of complete order. And one of them has partly clarified the intended meaning: 'The manager who drives the species from deficiency to perfection cannot but send prophets and establish laws, as is found in the world, so that order occurs, persons can live, and they can achieve the transition from deficiency to perfection for which they were created.'
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' (falāsifa) argument for the necessity of sending prophets, despite their denial of divine choice and particular knowledge. The argument is based on the concept of divine providence (al-ʿināya al-ilāhiyya): God's prior knowledge encompasses the best possible system of existents in their appropriate times. This knowledge necessitates the emanation of that system in reality, which includes the existence of a divinely revealed law (sharʿ) and a lawgiver (shāriʿ). Without a purpose-driven intention from God, the system still requires these elements for its perfect order. The author quotes from Avicenna's al-Shifāʾ, drawing an analogy: if divine providence provides lesser benefits like hair growth and foot arches, it must also provide what is essential for survival and the foundation of all good. Then the passage summarizes three ways of expressing this necessity: (1) in wisdom (ḥikma) – preserving order fittingly; (2) in providence (ʿināya) – the manifestation of order in God's comprehensive knowledge; (3) in nature (ṭabīʿa) – the existence of complete order. Finally, a clarifying view: the director who moves the species from deficiency to perfection necessarily sends prophets and lays down laws to achieve order, sustain life, and enable human perfection.

Key Terms
عناية إلهية
بعثة
نبوة
شرع
شارع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophers deny prophethood because they deny divine choice and particular knowledge.
Correction: They affirm prophethood as a necessary emanation from divine providence, even without divine will or intention.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy from al-Shifāʾ, if divine providence provides hair growth and foot arches, then it must also provide:
A) The existence of the law and the lawgiver.
B) The ability to fly.
C) Immediate knowledge of all things.
D) The survival of the fittest.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof for prophethood is rooted in the logical necessity of divine providence, not in divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If divine providence necessitates the law and lawgiver for the best order, does your daily life reflect a deep conviction that the divine law is not optional but essential for your survival and well-being? If not, what part of your life are you withholding from that order?
?
Avicenna argues that even seemingly trivial benefits like hair on eyelids are provided by divine providence. How does this affect the way you view the details of your religious obligations? Are you treating any command as beneath God's concern?
?
The philosophers deny that God acts for a purpose in inferior matters, yet they affirm prophethood through logical necessity. Does this challenge you to seek a more intellectual grounding for your faith, beyond mere feeling or tradition? If so, what step will you take this week to deepen your understanding?
